Tasmiyah: Deliberate vs. Forgetful Omission – Hanafi vs. Maliki
Inter-madhhab dispute: Malik's literalist argument vs Hanafi istihsan based on haraj

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school exempts forgetful omission of tasmiyah from prohibition and evaluate Malik's counter-argument based on literalism.

Core Idea
Forgetfulness removes blame: the Hanafi school exempts unintentional omission of tasmiyah to avoid hardship.

This passage presents a classic inter-madhhab dispute: Imam Malik argues from the literal generality of the Quran and hadith that any omission of tasmiyah, deliberate or forgetful, renders meat haram. The Hanafi school responds by introducing the principle of removing hardship (haraj) and noting that the existence of early scholarly disagreement indicates the text is not literal. Understanding this debate reveals how Hanafi usul uses istihsan and human forgetfulness to temper strict textualism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
What is the exact point of disagreement between Imam Malik and the Hanafis regarding the prohibition of meat without tasmiyah?
Focus on whether forgetfulness is excused.
2
State Malik's position and his proof
Malik holds that any omission, deliberate or forgetful, makes the meat haram. His proof is the literal (zahir) meaning of the Quranic verse and hadith, which make no distinction.
3
Analyze the Hanafi argument from hardship (haraj)
The Hanafi response argues that applying the literal meaning to forgetfulness would cause great hardship because people often forget. The Shariah removes hardship (raf' al-haraj), so forgetfulness is excused.
This is a classic application of istihsan.
4
Analyze the historical argument from ikhtilaf
The Hanafis further argue that if the verse were literal, the companions would have immediately resolved the issue. The fact that there was difference of opinion among them proves the verse is not taken literally.
This argument uses the absence of early consensus to infer non-literal interpretation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference: Hanafis permit eating meat slaughtered without tasmiyah if the omission was forgetful; Malikis prohibit it in all cases.
This affects daily practice for millions of Muslims.

Source Text

امام مالک کا نقطہ نظر

و مالك يحتج بظاهر ما ذكرنا إذ لا فضل فيه ولكنا نقول فى اعتبار ذالك من الحرج ما لا يخفى لان الانسان كثير النسيان والحرج مدفوع والسمع غير مجرى على ظاهره إذ لو اريد به لجرت المحاجة وظهر الانقياد وارتفع الخلاف في الصدر الاول

ترجمہ اور مالک استدلال کرتے ہیں ان دلائل سے جو ہم نے ذکر کی ہیں اس لئے کہ اس میں کوئی تفصیل نہیں ہے لیکن ہم کہیں گے کہ اس کے اعتبار کرنے میں وہ حرج جو پوشیدہ نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ انسان بڑا بھول بھلکڑ ہے اور حرج کو دور کیا گیا ہے اور دلیل نقلی اپنے ظاہر پر جاری نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ اگر اس سے ظاہر مراد ہوتا تو سلف میں محاجہ جاری ہوتا اور ( دوسری جانب سے ) تسلیم ظاہر ہوتی اور پہلے ہی زمانے میں اختلاف دور ہو گیا ہوتا۔

تشریح امام مالک فرماتے ہیں کہ آپ نے جو آیت اور حدیث پیش کی ہے اس کا ظاہر بتاتا ہے کہ اگر تسمیہ نہ کہا جائے خواہ عمدا یا نسیانا تو ذبیحہ حرام ہے کھایا نہیں جائے گا کیوں کہ ان میں عمد و نسیان کی کوئی تفصیل بیان نہیں کی گئی ہے ہم نے کہا اگر نسیان کے عذر کو عذر شمار نہ کیا جائے تو حرج کثیر لازم آئے گا حالانکہ شریعت حرج کو دور کرتی ہے اور انسان بھولتا بہت ہے تو پھر عظیم نقصان لازم آئے گا جیسا کہ ظاہر ہے

نیز اگر آیت کے ظاہری معنی مراد ہوتے تو صحابہ میں سے جو حضرات متروک التسمیہ ناسیا کی حرمت کے قائل تھے وہ دوسرے حضرات پر اس آیت سے حجت پکڑتے ۔ اور چونکہ یہ نص قطعی ہے دوسرا اس سے انکار نہیں کر سکتا تھا اور تسلیم کرتا اور جھگڑا صحابہ کے دور میں ختم ہو گیا ہوتا ۔ معلوم ہوا کہ آیت سے ظاہری معنی مراد نہیں ہے۔
Malik's viewpoint: And Malik argues by the literal meaning of what we mentioned, as there is no specification in it. But we say: In considering that, there is a hardship that is not hidden, because man is very forgetful, and hardship is removed. And the textual evidence is not applied literally, because if it were intended literally, then disputation would have occurred, submission would have appeared, and the difference would have been removed in the early period. Translation: And Malik argues by these proofs we have mentioned, because there is no detail in them. But we will say: In considering that, there is a hardship that is not hidden, because man is very forgetful, and hardship is removed. And the transmitted evidence is not applied literally, because if the literal meaning were intended, then disputation would have occurred among the early generations, and submission would have appeared, and the difference would have been removed in the first era. Commentary: Imam Malik says that the verse and hadith you presented indicate that if tasmiyah is not said, whether deliberately or forgetfully, the slaughtered animal is forbidden and cannot be eaten, because no distinction between deliberate and forgetful is mentioned. We said: If forgetfulness is not considered an excuse, great hardship would result, whereas the Shariah removes hardship, and man forgets a lot, so great loss would occur. Also, if the literal meaning of the verse were intended, then those companions who believed in the prohibition of meat without tasmiyah in forgetfulness would have argued against others using this verse. And since it is a definitive text, the other could not deny it and would submit, and the dispute would have ended in the era of the companions. It is understood that the literal meaning is not intended.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection of Imam Malik, who argued that the Quranic verse and hadith are general and do not distinguish between deliberate and forgetful omission of tasmiyah. According to Malik, any omission, whether deliberate or forgetful, renders the meat haram. The Hanafi response is that applying this generality would cause undue hardship (haraj), because people often forget. Since the Shariah is based on removing hardship, the ruling is restricted to deliberate omission only. Additionally, if the literal meaning were intended, the companions would have debated and resolved the issue early on, but the fact that there was difference of opinion among them indicates that the verse is not taken literally. This is a classic use of istihsan (juristic preference) over qiyas.

Key Terms
حرج (haraj): Hardship or difficulty that the Shariah aims to remove.
نسیان (nisyan): Forgetfulness; a legal excuse that removes liability.
ظاهر (zahir): Literal or apparent meaning of a text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response to Malik, why is the prohibition not applied to forgetful omission?
A) Because the verse is abrogated
B) Because of hardship (haraj)
C) Because the hadith is weak
D) Because Malik's view is rejected

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school limits the prohibition of eating meat without tasmiyah to deliberate omission, using the principle of removing hardship (haraj) and the historical presence of ikhtilaf among early scholars.

Shafi'i's Argument on Millah Substituting Tasmiyah and Its Refutation
Refutes Shafi'i claim that being a Muslim substitutes for tasmiyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between deliberate and forgetful omission of tasmiyah in Hanafi law and refute the Shafi'i analogy on millah substitution.

Core Idea
Millah substitutes for tasmiyah only for the excused (forgetful); deliberate omission breaks the ruling.

This passage takes up Imam Shafi'i's claim that a Muslim's faith (millah) automatically substitutes for the verbal tasmiyah, making meat halal even if the tasmiyah is deliberately omitted. The Hanafi response hinges on the concept of 'udhr (excuse): only the forgetful person is excused, so the substitution applies only to them. Deliberate omission leaves no excuse, so the meat becomes haram. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping how Hanafi fiqh prioritises intention and excuse over mere identity.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: deliberate omission of tasmiyah renders the meat haram (prohibited).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that millah substitutes for tasmiyah even in deliberate cases, so the meat remains halal.
This is the opposing position being refuted.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Shafi'i uses qiyas (analogy): if millah substitutes in forgetfulness, it should also substitute in deliberate cases.
The analogy assumes the same effective cause (illa).
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi reply distinguishes the effective cause: substitution occurs because of the excuse (udhr). Deliberate omission lacks excuse, so the analogy fails.
The hadiths cited by Shafi'i are interpreted as referring to forgetfulness, supported by consensus.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: Hanafi – meat is haram if tasmiyah deliberately omitted; Shafi'i – meat remains halal if slaughterer is Muslim.
This affects fatwa in cases of known deliberate omission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امام شافعی کے استدلال کا جواب

والاقامة في حق الناس وهو معذور لايدل عليها فى حق العامد ولا عذروما رواه محمول على حالة النسيان

ترجمہ اور رکھ دینا ( ملت کو تسمیہ کی جگہ ) ناسی کے حق میں حالانکہ وہ معذور ہے دلالت نہیں کرتا اقامت میں عامد کے حق میں حالانکہ کوئی عذر نہیں ہے اور جس کو شافعی نے روایت کیا ہے وہ نسیان کی حالت پر محمول ہے۔

تشریح امام شافعی نے فرمایا تھا کہ جیسے نسیان کی صورت میں ملت توحید تسمیہ کے قائم مقام ہوگئی ہے ایسے ہی عمد کی صورت میں ملت توحید تسمیہ کے قائم مقام ہو جائے گی۔

تو اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ ناسی تو معذور ہے اور عامد معذور نہیں ہے اور اقامت مذکورہ عذر کی وجہ سے ہوئی تھی تو جہاں عذر نہیں وہاں نیابت بھی نہ ہوگی اور امام شافعی نے جو دلیل ذکر کی تھی یعنی احادیث وہ نسیان کی حالت پر محمول ہے، کیوں ؟ اجماع کی دلیل
The response to Imam Shafi'i's argument: The substitution (of millah for tasmiyah) in the case of the forgetful person, who is excused, does not indicate it in the case of the deliberate person, who has no excuse. And what he narrated is carried on the state of forgetfulness. Translation: And the substitution (of the religion for tasmiyah) in the case of the forgetful person, while he is excused, is not evidence for it in the case of the deliberate person, while there is no excuse. And what Shafi'i narrated is carried on the state of forgetfulness. Commentary: Imam Shafi'i had said: Just as in the case of forgetfulness, the religion of Tawheed has taken the place of tasmiyah, similarly in the case of the deliberate, the religion of Tawheed would take the place of tasmiyah. The response to this is that the forgetful person is excused, while the deliberate is not excused, and the substitution mentioned was due to the excuse. Therefore, where there is no excuse, there will be no substitution. And the proof that Shafi'i mentioned, i.e., the hadiths, is carried on the state of forgetfulness, because of the consensus proof.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
Imam Shafi'i argued that a Muslim's faith (millah) substitutes for the verbal tasmiyah, so even if a Muslim deliberately forgets to say Bismillah, the meat is still halal. The Hanafi response distinguishes between forgetful and deliberate omission: the forgetful person has an excuse (nisyan), and the millah substitutes only in that case. For the deliberate person, there is no excuse, so the substitution does not apply. Additionally, the hadiths that Shafi'i relied upon are interpreted as referring to forgetfulness, as indicated by the consensus (ijma) on the prohibition in cases of deliberate omission.

Key Terms
اقامة (iqamah): Substitution; here, the religion (millah) taking the place of tasmiyah.
ملت (millah): Religion or faith; specifically Islam and Tawheed.
عذر (udhr): Legal excuse that exempts one from liability or obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi response to Shafi'i's argument that a Muslim's millah substitutes for tasmiyah?
A) The millah only substitutes for forgetful people, not deliberate ones
B) The millah never substitutes
C) The hadiths are unreliable
D) Both positions are correct

Key Takeaway
Millah substitutes for tasmiyah only in cases of forgetfulness due to the presence of a valid excuse; deliberate omission renders the meat prohibited in the Hanafi school.

Requirement of Tasmiyah at Slaughter vs Hunting
Explains when tasmiyah is required for slaughter vs hunting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when tasmiyah is required for slaughter of domestic animals versus hunting, and apply the rule to determine the permissibility of meat in given scenarios.

Core Idea
Tasmiyah is required at the action under your control: at slaughter for domestic animals, at release/shoot for hunting.

This passage clarifies the precise moment and object of tasmiyah for two types of slaughter: domestic (ikhtiyar) and hunting (sayd). The ruling hinges on what the person can control. Understanding this distinction prevents common errors in halal meat certification and hunting practices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of slaughter
Separate domestic animal slaughter (dhakat al-ikhtiyar) from hunting (sayd). Note that for domestic slaughter, the controlling action is the act of slaughter itself; for hunting, it is the release or shot.
2
Determine the moment tasmiyah is required
For domestic slaughter, tasmiyah must be said at the time of slaughter over the animal. For hunting, it must be said at the time of releasing the dog/falcon or shooting the arrow, over the tool (dog, arrow).
3
Understand the rationale: maqdoor 'alayhi
The tasmiyah is tied to the action the person can control. In slaughter, one controls the cut; in hunting, one controls the release/shot, not the hitting of the game.
This explains why tasmiyah on the wrong arrow invalidates the meat.
4
Apply the rule to the given cases
Work through each example: tasmiyah on one sheep slaughtered another → invalid; arrow tasmiyah hits unintended game → valid; arrow tasmiyah but different arrow shot → invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم التسمية فى ذكاة الاختيار تشترط عند الذبح وهو على المذبوح وفي الصيد تشترط عند الارسال والرمى وهو على الآلة لان المقدور له فى الأول الذبح وفى الثانى الرمي والارسال دون الاصابة فيشترط عند فعل يقدر عليه حتى اذا اضجع شاة وسمى فذبح غيرها بتلك التسمية لا يجوز ولو رمي الى صيد وسمى و اصاب غيره حل وكذا فى الارسال ولو اضجع شاة وسمى ثم رمى بالشفرة وذبح بأخرى أكل ولو سمى على سهم ثم رمى بغيره صيداً لا يوكل

ترجمہ پھر تسمیہ ذکاۃ اختیاری میں ذبح کے وقت شرط ہے اور یہ تسمیہ مذبوح پر ہوگا اور شکار میں ارسال اور پھینکنے کے وقت شرط ہے اور دوسری صورت میں پھینکنا اور چھوڑنا ہے نہ کہ شکار پکڑ لینا تو تسمیہ ایسے فعل کے وقت شرط ہو گا جس کے اوپر وہ قادر ہو یہاں تک کہ جب اس نے کسی بکری کو لٹایا اور تسمیہ کہا پھر اس تسمیہ سے اس کے غیر کو ذبح کر دیا تو جائز نہیں اور اگر شکار کی جانب تیر پھینکا اور تسمیہ کہا اور اس کے غیر کو لگ گیا تو حلال ہے اور ایسے ہی چھوڑنے میں (یعنی کتا اور باز چھوڑنے میں ) اور اگر بکری کو لٹایا اور تسمیہ کہا پھر چھری کو پھینک
Then tasmiyah in optional slaughter is required at the time of slaughter, and it is over the slaughtered animal. And in hunting, it is required at the time of releasing and throwing, and it is over the tool, because what is within one's power in the first is slaughter, and in the second is throwing and releasing, not hitting. So it is required at the action he is capable of. Thus, if he lays down a sheep and says tasmiyah, then slaughters another with that tasmiyah, it is not permissible. And if he shoots an arrow at game and says tasmiyah, and it hits another, it is permissible. Similarly in releasing. And if he lays down a sheep and says tasmiyah, then throws a knife and slaughters with another, he may eat. And if he says tasmiyah over an arrow, then shoots another arrow at game, it is not eaten. Translation: Then tasmiyah in optional slaughter is a condition at the time of slaughter, and this tasmiyah is over the slaughtered animal. And in hunting, it is a condition at the time of releasing and throwing, and it is over the tool, because in the first, what is within his power is slaughter, and in the second, it is throwing and releasing, not striking. So it is a condition at the action over which he has control. So much so that if he lays down a sheep and says tasmiyah, then slaughters another with that tasmiyah, it is not permissible. And if he shoots an arrow at game and says tasmiyah, and it hits another, it is halal. Similarly in releasing (i.e., releasing a dog or falcon). And if he lays down a sheep and says tasmiyah, then throws a knife and slaughters with another, he may eat. And if he says tasmiyah over an arrow, then shoots another arrow at game, it is not eaten (that game).
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage clarifies the application of tasmiyah. For domestic animals slaughtered voluntarily (dhabh ikhtiyari), the tasmiyah must be said at the moment of slaughter, directly over the animal. If one says tasmiyah on one sheep but slaughters another, that meat is not permissible because the tasmiyah was not associated with that animal. For hunting, tasmiyah is required at the time of releasing the dog or throwing the arrow, because that is the action under the hunter's control. If the arrow hits unintended game, it is halal because the tasmiyah was validly said at the launch. But if he says tasmiyah on one arrow and uses a different arrow, the game is not halal. This distinction is based on the principle that tasmiyah must accompany the action one can control.

Key Terms
ذكاة الاختيار (dhakat al-ikhtiyar): Voluntary/optional slaughter of domestic animals, as opposed to hunting (dhakat al-idtirar).
صيد (sayd): Hunting; the act of capturing or killing wild game.
ارسال (irsal): Releasing a hunting animal (dog, falcon) towards game.
رمى (ramy): Throwing or shooting a projectile (arrow, bullet) at game.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a hunter says tasmiyah over an arrow but then shoots a different arrow at game, is the game halal?
A) Yes, because tasmiyah was said
B) No, because tasmiyah must be on the specific arrow used
C) Yes, if he intended the same game
D) No, only if he says tasmiyah directly on the game

Key Takeaway
Tasmiyah is required at the action under your control: at slaughter for domestic animals, at release or shot for hunting.

Three Cases of Mentioning Other than Allah at Slaughter
Expounds three rulings on mentioning other names with Allah at slaughter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three scenarios of mentioning other than Allah at slaughter, state the ruling (makruh tahrimi, haram, mubah) for each, and explain the grammatical basis for the difference.

Core Idea
The ruling depends on the grammatical connection: mawsul without conjunction (makruh), ma'tuf with conjunction (haram), separate supplication (mubah).

This lesson examines three distinct rulings from Imam Muhammad regarding mentioning other than Allah at the time of slaughter. The critical factor is not the mere mention but how it is phrased: whether the other name is attached directly without a conjunction, linked with a conjunction ('wa'), or stated entirely separately. This grammatical distinction determines whether the meat remains halal (but disliked), becomes haram, or is perfectly permissible.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the three grammatical constructions
The passage identifies three ways to mention another name: (1) mawsul without 'wa' (conjoined), (2) ma'tuf with 'wa' (with conjunction), and (3) mafsul (completely separate in form and meaning). Identify each pattern in the Arabic examples.
The first is 'Bismillah Muhammad Rasul Allah', the second is 'Bismillah wa ismi fulaan', the third is a supplication before or after tasmiyah.
2
Apply the Hanafi principle of tasmiyah exclusivity
The Hanafi school holds that tasmiyah must be exclusive to Allah at the moment of slaughter. Any sense of partnership invalidates the slaughter. Here, 'exclusive' means no other being is associated in the dedication.
3
Determine the hukm for each case
First case: makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) because it resembles partnership but does not actualize it. Second case: haram because explicit conjunction creates partnership. Third case: mubah (permissible) because it is separate.
The meat of the first case is halal but disliked; the second is haram; the third is fully halal.
4
Examine the prophetic proof for the third case
A hadith shows the Prophet saying 'Allahumma taqbal hadhihi...' after slaughter, indicating that a separate supplication does not affect tasmiyah.
This hadith is the dalil for permissibility.
5
Note the cross-madhhab dispute
Imam al-Shafi'i in his later view considers any mention of another name, even without conjunction, as nullifying tasmiyah, making the animal haram. The Hanafi position relies on the grammatical distinction.
The practical difference: Hanafis allow the first case as makruh, Shafi'is would hold it haram.

اللہ تعالیٰ کے نام کے ساتھ غیر اللہ کا نام لینا ذبح کے وقت مکروہ ہے کے متعلق پہلا مسئلہ
قال ويكره ان يذكر مع اسم الله تعالى شيئا غيره وان يقول عند الذبح اللهم تقبل من فلان وهذه ثلث مسائل إحدهما ان يذكر موصولاً لا معطوفا فيكره ولا تحرم الذبيحة وهو المراد بما قال ونظيره ان يقول بسم الله محمد رسول الله لان الشركة لم توجد فلم يكن الذبح واقعا له الا انه يكره لوجود القران صورة فيتصور بصورة المحرم
ترجمہ امام محمد نے فرمایا، اور مکروہ ہے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ کے نام کے ساتھ اس کے علاوہ کسی شئی کا ذکر کرے اور مکروہ ہے یہ کہ وہ ذبح کے وقت کہے اے اللہ فلاں کی جانب سے قبول فرمالے اور تین مسائل ہیں ان میں سے ایک یہ ہے کہ وہ موصلاً ذکر کرے نہ کہ معطوف بنا کر پس یہ مکروہ ہے اور ذبیحہ حرام نہیں ہے اور امام محمد نے جو فرمایا ہے (متن میں ) اس سے یہی مراد ہے اور اس کی نظیر ہے یہ کہ وہ کہے بسم اللہ محمد رسول اللہ ( یعنی مکروہ ہے حرام نہیں ہے ) اس لئے کہ شرکت نہیں پائی گئی تو ذبیح آپ صلى الله عليه وسلم کے لئے نہ ہوگا مگر یہ مکروہ ہے صورۃ قرآن (ملاپ) کے پائے جانے کی وجہ سے تو یہ حرام کی صورت میں متشکل ہوگا۔

دوسرا مسئلہ
والثانية ان يذكر موصولا على وجه العطف والشركة بان يقول بسم الله واسم فلان او يقول بسم الله وفلان الله ومحمد رسول الله بكسر الدال فتحرم الذبيحة لانه اهل به لـ لغير الله
ترجمہ اور دوسرا مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ موصلاً عطف اور شرکت کے طریقہ پر (غیر کا ذکر کر کے اس طریقہ پر کہے کہ بسم اللہ واسم فلاں یا کہے بسم الله وفلاں یا ، بسم اللہ محمد رسول اللہ وال کے کسرہ کے ساتھ پس ذبیحہ حرام ہے اس لئے کہ اس پر غیر اللہ کا نام پکارا گیا ہے۔

تیسرا مسئلہ
والثالثة ان يقول مفصولا عنه صورة ومعنى بان يقول قبل التسمية وقبل ان يضجع الذبيحة او بعده وهذا لاباس به لما روى عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم انه قال بعد الذبح اللهم تقبل هذه عن امة محمد ممن شهد لك بالوحدانية ولى بالبلاغ
ترجمہ اور تیسرا مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ وہ کہے صورت اور معنی دونوں اعتبار سے ذکر اللہ سے الگ اس طریقہ پر کہ وہ کہے تسمیہ سے پہلے اور ذبیحہ کولٹانے سے پہلے یا اس کے بعد اور یہ وہ صورت ہے جس میں کوئی جرم نہیں ہے بوجہ اس کے کہ نبی صلى الله عليه وسلم سے مروی ہے کہ آپ نے ذبح کے بعد فرمایا اے اللہ اس کو قبول فرما محمد کی امت کی طرف سے ان لوگوں کی طرف سے جنہوں نے تیرے لئے وحدانیت کی گواہی دی ہو اور میرے لئے پہنچانے کی۔
First issue regarding it being disliked to mention something other than Allah's name at the time of slaughter: He (Imam Muhammad) said: It is disliked to mention anything other than Allah's name along with it, and it is disliked to say at the time of slaughter: 'O Allah, accept from so-and-so.' And these are three issues: The first is that he mentions it conjoined (mawsulan) not with a conjunction (ma'tuf), so it is disliked but the slaughtered animal is not unlawful. That is what is intended by his statement. And its analogy is that he says 'Bismillah Muhammad Rasul Allah' because partnership (shirk) is not found, so the slaughter is not for him (the Prophet), but it is disliked due to the apparent conjunction (qiran) in form, so it takes the appearance of the unlawful. Translation: Imam Muhammad said: It is disliked to mention anything other than Allah along with the name of Allah, and it is disliked that he say at the time of slaughter: 'O Allah, accept from so-and-so.' And there are three issues: one of them is that he mentions it conjoined (mawsulan) not as a conjunction (ma'tuf), so this is disliked and the slaughtered animal is not unlawful. What Imam Muhammad said (in the matn) means this. And its analogy is that he says 'Bismillah Muhammad Rasul Allah' (meaning it is disliked, not unlawful) because partnership is not found, so the slaughtered animal would not be for the Prophet (peace be upon him), but it is disliked due to the apparent conjunction (qiran) in form, so it takes the appearance of the unlawful. Second issue: And the second is that he mentions it conjoined (mawsulan) in the manner of conjunction and partnership, such that he says 'Bismillah wa ismi fulaan' or he says 'Bismillah wa fulaan' or 'Bismillah wa Muhammad Rasul Allah' with the kasra on the dal (of 'wa'), then the slaughtered animal is unlawful because it has been dedicated to other than Allah. Translation: And the second issue is that he mentions it conjoined in the manner of conjunction and partnership, such that he says 'Bismillah wa ismi fulaan' or 'Bismillah wa fulaan' or 'Bismillah wa Muhammad Rasul Allah' with the kasra on the 'wa', so the slaughtered animal is unlawful because upon it the name of other than Allah has been invoked. Third issue: And the third is that he says it separate (mafsulan) from it in form and meaning, such that he says it before the tasmiyah and before he lays down the animal or after it, and this is fine because it is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said after slaughter: 'O Allah, accept this from the ummah of Muhammad, from those who testify to Your oneness and to my conveying (the message).' Translation: And the third issue is that he says it separate from the remembrance of Allah in both form and meaning, in such a way that he says it before the tasmiyah and before he lays down the animal or after it, and this is the case in which there is no harm because it is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said after slaughter: 'O Allah, accept this from the ummah of Muhammad, from those who testify to Your oneness and to my conveying.'
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah explains three rulings regarding mentioning other than Allah at the time of slaughter. Building on Imam Muhammad's statement that it is disliked to mention anything else with Allah's name, the author identifies three distinct scenarios. FIRST CASE: The person mentions another name in a conjoined manner without a conjunction (e.g., 'Bismillah Muhammad Rasul Allah'). Here, no actual partnership is intended because the other name is not grammatically linked to Allah's name. The slaughter remains lawful (halal) but it is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) because of the apparent association. SECOND CASE: The person uses a conjunction (e.g., 'Bismillah wa ismi fulaan' or 'Bismillah wa Muhammad Rasul Allah' with the waw in the accusative). This explicitly partners another with Allah, making the animal haram (unlawful) because it has been dedicated to other than Allah. THIRD CASE: The person makes a separate supplication before or after the tasmiyah, not connected to it (e.g., 'Allahumma taqbal min fulaan' before slaughter or after). This is permissible and not disliked because it is separate in both form and meaning, supported by the Prophet's practice of praying after slaughter. The ruling is based on the principle that tasmiyah must be exclusive to Allah at the moment of slaughter; any partnership renders the meat unlawful.

Key Terms
موصول (mawsul): Conjoined: mentioning another name directly attached to Allah's name without a conjunction word. Example: 'Bismillah Muhammad Rasul Allah'.
معطوف (ma'tuf): With a conjunction (wa): explicitly linking another name to Allah's name, indicating partnership. Example: 'Bismillah wa ismi fulaan'.
مفصول (mafsul): Separate: mentioning another name completely apart from tasmiyah, in form and meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone says 'Bismillah wa Muhammad Rasul Allah' (with kasra on wa) while slaughtering a chicken, what is the ruling?
A) The chicken is halal but makruh.
B) The chicken is haram.
C) The chicken is halal without any dislike.
D) The chicken is halal only if the person intended partnership.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of slaughter meat depends on whether the mention of another name is conjoined without conjunction (makruh), with conjunction (haram), or entirely separate (mubah).

Mentioning Another Name Without Grammatical Conjunction at Slaughter
Ruling on mentioning another name without 'atf at slaughter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when another name is mentioned alongside Allah without 'atf at the time of slaughter and explain why the meat remains halal.

Core Idea
Mentioning a name without 'atf does not make meat haram; it is makruh but halal.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a specific scenario during slaughter: the slaughterer utters the tasmiyah and adds the Prophet's name without using the word 'and'. The key distinction is the absence of grammatical conjunction ('atf). The ruling is that such an utterance is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) because it outwardly resembles associating a partner, but the meat is halal because no actual partnership is intended. This ruling is based on Imam Muhammad's transmission in al-Jami' al-Saghir, and al-Marghinani expands it in al-Hidayah, providing the proofs behind both aspects of the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is mentioning another name (e.g., the Prophet Muhammad) alongside Allah's name during the tasmiyah of dhabihah without using 'atf. The ruling: the utterance is makruh tahrimi, but the slaughtered animal remains halal.
Makruh tahrimi is close to haram but not fully haram due to less definitive proof.
2
Understand the absence of partnership
The proof for halal is that the Prophet is not made a partner in the slaughter. If partnership were intended, the name would be in the genitive case (majrur), e.g., 'Bismillah Muhammadin' (meaning 'in the name of Allah and Muhammad'), but the actual utterance 'Bismillah Muhammad Rasul Allah' is not genitive, so no partnership exists.
Grammatical analysis shows the intention, not just the words.
3
Analyze the dalil for makruh
The proof for makruh is that the mere outward conjunction of another name with Allah's name resembles shirk. To avoid this resemblance and uphold caution, the action is declared makruh tahrimi, even though the meat is halal.
This is a precautionary measure (ihtiyat) to safeguard the sanctity of Allah's name.
4
Identify the authority level
This ruling comes from Imam Muhammad's narration in al-Jami' al-Saghir (a zahir al-riwayah text). Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah endorses it, making it the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).
Zahir al-riwayah is the most authoritative level in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ریح امام محمد نے جامع صغیر میں فرمایا ہے کہ اللہ کے نام کے ساتھ دوسرے کو ذکر کرنا مکروہ ہے اور بوقت ذبح یوں کہنا مکروہ ہے اللهم تقبل من فلان اس پر صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ یہاں تین مسائل ہیں۔
دوسرے کا ذکر کیا جائے لیکن عطف کے طریقہ پر نہیں بلکہ وصل و ملاپ کے طریقہ پر بغیر عطف کے تو یہ مکروہ ہے لیکن ذبیحہ حلال رہے گا اور امام محمد کی اپنے فرمان الہی سے یہی مذکور ہے ۔ اس پہلی صورت کی مثال یہ ہے کہ ذابح کہے بسم اللہ محمد رسول اللہ تو یہ مکروہ ہے اور ذبیحہ حلال ہے۔ حلال کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہاں آپ. کو شریک نہیں کیا گیا اور اگر شریک بنانا مقصود ہوتا تو پھر مجد مجرور ہوتا۔ بہر حال جب اس میں شرکت نہیں پائی گئی تو ذبیح آپ صلى الله عليه وسلم کے لئے نہ ہوا۔ اور مکروہ ہونے کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہاں صورۃ اللہ کے نام کے ساتھ غیر کا قرآن اور ملاپ ہے تو احتیاط کا تقاضہ حرمت کا ہے اس وجہ سے اس کو مکروہ تحریمی قرار دیا گیا ہے اس کو مصنف نے کہا ہے کہ یہ حرام کی شکل میں متشکل ہوگا ۔
Imam Muhammad narrated in al-Jami' al-Saghir that mentioning someone else along with Allah's name is makruh, and at the time of slaughter saying ‘Allahumma taqabbal min fulan’ is makruh. Upon this, the author of al-Hidayah says there are three issues here. [First:] If someone else is mentioned but not in the manner of 'atf (conjunction), rather in the manner of connection and association without 'atf, then it is makruh but the slaughtered animal remains halal, and this is what Imam Muhammad mentioned from his ruling. An example of this first case is that the slaughterer says ‘Bismillah Muhammad Rasul Allah’ – this is makruh and the slaughtered animal is halal. The proof for halal is that the Prophet (peace be upon him) is not made a partner here, and if partnership was intended, then [the name] would be in the genitive. In any case, when no partnership is found, the slaughter is not for the Prophet. And the proof for it being makruh is that here there is outward conjunction and association of another with Allah's name, so caution requires [considering it] haram; for this reason it is declared makruh tahrimi. The author said it resembles haram in form.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage presents the first of three issues (masa'il) regarding mentioning someone other than Allah during the tasmiyah at slaughter. The ruling is that if the slaughterer mentions another name (e.g., the Prophet Muhammad) alongside Allah but without grammatical conjunction ('atf) – meaning he simply utters them together without the word 'and' – then the slaughter is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) but the meat is halal. The reasoning is that the additional name is not made a partner in the dedication; the slaughter is still solely for Allah. However, because it outwardly resembles associating a partner, it is deemed makruh tahrimi to avoid the appearance of shirk. Imam Muhammad's position from al-Jami' al-Saghir is cited as the basis.

Key Terms
عطف ('atf): Grammatical conjunction using 'and' (waw) linking two names as partners.
مکروہ تحریمی (makruh tahrimi): An act that is prohibited but with a less than definitive proof, close to haram.
ذبیحہ (dhabihah): The animal that is slaughtered according to Islamic rites.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first issue in al-Hidayah, if a slaughterer says 'Bismillah Muhammad Rasul Allah' without 'atf, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) Haram, meat not permissible
B) Makruh tahrimi, meat halal
C) Makruh tanzihi, meat halal
D) Permissible without dislike

Key Takeaway
Mentioning another name without grammatical conjunction is makruh tahrimi but does not affect the halal status of the meat.

Second Issue: Mentioning Another With 'Atf (Partner)
Ruling on mentioning another as partner using 'atf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that if the slaughterer mentions another name with the conjunction 'and' (عطف), the slaughtered animal is haram, and explain that it falls under the Qur'anic prohibition of 'uhilla li ghayrillah'.

Core Idea
Conjunction with 'and' makes the other a partner in dedication, invoking other than Allah, thus haram.

In this lesson, we examine the second scenario mentioned in al-Hidayah regarding the name invoked during slaughter. The key is whether the other name is mentioned as a partner (with 'atf) or simply mentioned without partnership. The ruling hinges on the presence of the conjunction, which transforms the utterance into a shirk in the dedication, making the meat haram.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance pattern
Recognize that the slaughterer says something like 'Bismillah wa ismi fulan' or 'Bismillah wa fulan' — the key is the conjunction 'wa' linking another name to Allah's name.
2
Identify the presence of 'atf (conjunction)
The 'wa' (and) explicitly connects the other name as a partner in the act of dedication. This is the critical element that triggers the ruling.
The grammatical construction indicates partnership.
3
Apply the ruling
Because the conjunction makes the dedication to other than Allah, the slaughtered animal becomes haram and cannot be consumed.
The ruling is absolute: haram, not makruh.
4
Understand the talil (reasoning)
The act falls under the Qur'anic prohibition in Surah al-Ma'idah (5:3) of 'uhilla li ghayrillah' — dedicated to other than Allah. The conjunction is the cause (illa) for the prohibition.
The reason is explicit in the text: 'li-annahu uhilla li ghayrillah'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثانية ان يذكر موصولا على وجه العطف والشركة بان يقول بسم الله واسم فلان او يقول بسم الله وفلان الله ومحمد رسول الله بكسر الدال فتحرم الذبيحة لانه اهل به لـ لغير الله
ترجمہ اور دوسرا مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ موصلاً عطف اور شرکت کے طریقہ پر (غیر کا ذکر کر کے اس طریقہ پر کہے کہ بسم اللہ واسم فلاں یا کہے
بسم الله وفلاں یا ، بسم اللہ محمد رسول اللہ وال کے کسرہ کے ساتھ پس ذبیحہ حرام ہے اس لئے کہ اس پر غیر اللہ کا نام پکارا گیا ہے۔ تشریح یہ مسائل ثلثہ مذکورہ میں سے دوسرے مسئلہ کا ذکر ہے جس میں غیر کا ذکر اللہ کا نام کے ساتھ کیا جائے اور عطف کے ساتھ کیا جائے جس کی امثلہ ظاہر ہیں کہ ان تمام مثالوں میں اللہ کے ساتھ دوسرے کو شریک کیا گیا ہے تو اس صورت میں ذبیحہ حرام ہے۔
And the second [issue] is that he mentions [it] connectedly in the manner of conjunction and partnership, such that he says 'Bismillah wa ismi fulan' or he says 'Bismillah wa fulan' or 'Bismillah Muhammad Rasul Allah' with the kasra of the dal (i.e., 'wa Muhammadin'?), so the slaughtered animal becomes haram because it is dedicated to other than Allah. Translation: And the second issue is that he mentions (another) connectedly in the manner of conjunction and partnership, such that he says 'Bismillah wa ismi fulan' or says 'Bismillah wa fulan' or 'Bismillah Muhammad Rasul Allah' with the kasra on the dal (i.e., making it genitive, thus partner), then the slaughtered animal is haram because the name of other than Allah is called upon it. Explanation: This is the mention of the second of the three issues, in which another is mentioned along with Allah's name and with conjunction, and examples are clear: in all these examples, another is made a partner with Allah, so in this case the slaughtered animal is haram.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage covers the second issue: if the slaughterer mentions another name with the conjunction 'and' (waw al-'atf), thereby making that name a partner in the dedication, the slaughtered animal is haram. The examples given include saying 'Bismillah wa ismi fulan' (in the name of Allah and the name of so-and-so), 'Bismillah wa fulan', or 'Bismillah wa Muhammad Rasul Allah' with the genitive case on 'Muhammad' indicating partnership. The key is that the conjunction explicitly links the other name as a partner in the act of dedication, thus the animal is slaughtered for other than Allah (uhilla li ghayrillah), which is explicitly prohibited in the Qur'an (5:3). Hence it is haram and the meat is impermissible.

Key Terms
أهل به لغير الله (uhilla bihi li ghayrillah): The animal is slaughtered invoking the name of other than Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
If the slaughterer mentions another name with the conjunction 'and', the meat is haram because it is dedicated to other than Allah.

Third Issue: Mentioning Another Separately (Mufsolan)
Ruling on mentioning another separately

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mentioning another separately at the time of slaughter and apply the condition of separation in form and meaning.

Core Idea
Mentioning another separately from the tasmiyah is permissible.

In this lesson, we examine the third issue regarding the mention of others during slaughter. The key distinction is whether the mention is connected to the basmalah or entirely separate. You will see that the Hanafi school permits it based on the Prophet's own practice, as long as it is 'mufsolan' – separate in both wording and meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Focus on the scenario: the slaughterer mentions a person (e.g., a supplication for someone) before, during, or after slaughter. The ruling here is that it is permissible (mubah) and not disliked, provided it is separate.
This is the third of three issues about mentioning other than Allah during slaughter.
2
Understand the condition of separation (mufsolan)
The condition is that the mention must be 'mufsolan' – completely separate in form and meaning from the tasmiyah. For example, saying it before the basmalah or after slaughter, without any conjunction or continuity.
If the mention is connected (maslan), it becomes makruh; if it is made part of the tasmiyah itself (shartan), it invalidates the slaughter.
3
Locate the dalil
The proof is from the Prophet's (peace be upon him) practice: after slaughter, he said, 'O Allah, accept this on behalf of the Ummah of Muhammad...' This shows that such a separate supplication is allowed.
The hadith is cited in the text as evidence for permissibility.
4
Apply the ruling to the condition
If the mention meets the condition of separation, the meat is halal and there is no harm. If not, the ruling changes according to the degree of connection.
The cross-madhhab difference: some schools may hold that any mention besides Allah is makruh even if separate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالثة ان يقول مفصولا عنه صورة ومعنى بان يقول قبل التسمية وقبل ان يضجع الذبيحة او بعده وهذا لاباس به لما روى عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم انه قال بعد الذبح اللهم تقبل هذه عن امة محمد ممن شهد لك بالوحدانية ولى بالبلاغ
ترجمہ اور تیسرا مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ وہ کہے صورت اور معنی دونوں اعتبار سے ذکر اللہ سے الگ اس طریقہ پر کہ وہ کہے تسمیہ سے پہلے اور ذبیحہ کولٹانے سے پہلے یا اس کے بعد اور یہ وہ صورت ہے جس میں کوئی جرم نہیں ہے بوجہ اس کے کہ نبی صلى الله عليه وسلم سے مروی ہے کہ آپ نے ذبح کے بعد فرمایا اے اللہ اس کو قبول فرما محمد کی امت کی طرف سے ان لوگوں کی طرف سے جنہوں نے تیرے لئے وحدانیت کی گواہی دی ہو اور میرے لئے پہنچانے کی۔
تشریح یہ مشائل ثلثہ مذکورہ میں سے تیسرا مسئلہ ہے اور یہ صورت جائز ہے اس میں غیر کا ذکر تو ہوتا ہے مگر اللہ کے ساتھ نہیں ہوتا بلکہ بالکل جدا ہوتا ہے۔
And the third [issue] is that he says [it] separated from [the name of Allah] in form and meaning, such that he says before the tasmiyah and before laying down the animal or after it, and there is no harm in this because of what is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said after slaughter: 'O Allah, accept this on behalf of the Ummah of Muhammad, from those who bear witness to Your oneness and to my conveying [the message].' Translation: And the third issue is that he says it separated from the remembrance of Allah in both form and meaning, in such a way that he says before the tasmiyah and before laying down the animal or after it, and this is a case in which there is no sin because of what is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said after slaughter: 'O Allah, accept this on behalf of the Ummah of Muhammad...' Explanation: This is the third of the three issues, and this case is permissible. In it, mention of another occurs, but not alongside Allah; rather it is completely separate.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This is the third issue: if the slaughterer mentions another person (e.g., saying 'Allahumma taqabbal min fulan') before the tasmiyah or before laying the animal down, or after the slaughter, completely separate from the invocation of Allah's name, then it is permissible (la ba'sa bihi). The proof is the hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) himself said after slaughter: 'Allahumma taqabbal hadhihi 'an ummati Muhammad...' This shows that such a supplication after slaughter is allowed. The key is that there is no conjunction or connection with the tasmiyah; the mention of another is entirely separate in both wording and meaning. Therefore, the meat is halal and there is no dislike.

Key Terms
مفصولاً (mufsolan): Separated in form and meaning, not connected to the tasmiyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third issue, when is it permissible to mention another person during slaughter?
A) Only after the animal is dead
B) When it is completely separate from the tasmiyah in form and meaning
C) Only if the person is a saint
D) Never, it is always haram

Key Takeaway
Mentioning another person during slaughter is permissible as long as it is completely separate from the tasmiyah in form and meaning.

The Condition of Pure Dhikr in Tasmiyah
Requirement that tasmiyah be pure remembrance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition that tasmiyah must be pure dhikr and identify which utterances fulfill or violate it.

Core Idea
Tasmiyah must be pure remembrance, not mixed with personal requests; intention determines validity.

When slaughtering an animal, the Hanafi school requires the tasmiyah (saying Bismillah) to be a pure act of remembrance. This lesson examines what qualifies as pure dhikr and what does not, based on the guidance of Ibn Mas'ud and the practice of the early Muslims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core condition
Start by recognising that the tasmiyah must be 'al-dhikr al-khalis' – pure remembrance, free from any personal request or worldly intent.
Ibn Mas'ud's instruction 'Jarridu al-tasmiyah' (make the tasmiyah pure) is the basis.
2
Distinguish between dhikr and dua
Understand that saying 'Allahumma ighfir li' is a supplication (dua) and therefore does not constitute pure dhikr. The meat becomes haram.
The key is whether the utterance is a request or pure praise.
3
Analyse the role of intention
If one says 'Alhamdulillah' or 'Subhanallah' with the specific intention of tasmiyah, it is valid. But if said as a response to sneezing, the intention is different and it fails.
The more sound narration (asahh al-riwayatain) holds the sneeze scenario invalid.
4
Apply to common practice
The phrase 'Bismillah wa Allahu akbar' is the established form, traced to Ibn Abbas, and fulfils the condition perfectly.
This shows the recommended wording includes both tasmiyah and takbir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the condition is the pure, unadulterated remembrance, as Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'Make the tasmiyah pure.' Even if he says at the time of slaughter 'Allahumma ighfir li', it is not permissible because it is a supplication and request. If he says 'Alhamdulillah' or 'Subhanallah' intending the tasmiyah, it is permissible. And if he sneezes at the time of slaughter and says 'Alhamdulillah', it is not valid according to the more sound of the two narrations, because he intends by it praise of Allah for a blessing, not the tasmiyah. And what is commonly on people's tongues at the time of slaughter, i.e., saying 'Bismillah wa Allahu akbar', is transmitted from Ibn 'Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them both) regarding the saying of Allah: 'So mention the name of Allah upon them in rows.'
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage establishes the condition that the tasmiyah must be pure dhikr (remembrance) without any admixture of personal supplication or request. It begins with the statement of Ibn Mas'ud: 'Jarridu al-tasmiyah' (Make the tasmiyah pure). Four scenarios are discussed: (1) If one says 'Allahumma ighfir li' at the time of slaughter, it is not valid because it is a dua (supplication). The meat is not halal. (2) If one says 'Alhamdulillah' or 'Subhanallah' with the intention of tasmiyah, it is valid because these are forms of dhikr and one intended by them the tasmiyah. (3) If one sneezes and says 'Alhamdulillah' (as a response to sneezing), it is not valid according to the more sound narration because the intention is praise for the blessing of sneezing, not the tasmiyah. (4) The common practice of saying 'Bismillah wa Allahu akbar' is traced to Ibn 'Abbas as an interpretation of the verse 'fa-udhkur isma Allahi 'alayha sawaff' (Quran 22:36). This implies that saying 'Allahu akbar' along with tasmiyah is recommended.

Key Terms
الذكر الخالص (al-dhikr al-khalis): Pure remembrance of Allah without any admixture of personal supplication or worldly intent.
تسمیہ (tasmiyah): The invocation of Allah's name at the time of slaughter, typically 'Bismillah, Allahu Akbar'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) No, because sneezing invalidates the slaughter.

Key Takeaway
For validity of slaughter, the tasmiyah must be pure dhikr, free from personal requests, and the intention must be solely to perform the sunnah.

Place of Slaughter: Between Throat and Collar
Ruling on the permissible area for slaughter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the permissible area for slaughter according to the Hanafi school and identify the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
The valid slaughter (al-dhakah) must be between the collar and the jawbones; the entire throat is acceptable.

In this passage, Al-Marghinani addresses a critical condition for halal slaughter: the exact location of the cut. While the text from Al-Hidayah initially seems restrictive, the clarification from Al-Jami' al-Saghir expands the permissible area to the entire throat. Understanding this ruling ensures that the act of slaughter fulfills its legal purpose.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is slaughter (dhabh). The ruling is that a specific area is a condition (shart) for validity; otherwise the animal does not become halal.
2
Locate the evidence from hadith
The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'al-dhakah ma bayna al-labbah wa al-lahyayn' (the valid slaughter is between the collar and the jawbones). This defines the boundaries.
The hadith is the primary dalil for this ruling.
3
Note the expansion from Al-Jami' al-Saghir
Al-Jami' al-Saghir states: 'There is no harm in slaughter in the entire throat: its middle, top, and bottom.' This clarifies that any part of the throat within the boundaries is acceptable.
The mufta bihi is the expansive view.
4
Understand the rationale
The area between the collar and jawbones contains the major veins and the windpipe, so cutting there ensures proper bleeding and death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال والذبح بين الحلق واللبة وفى الجامع الصغير لاباس بالدبح في الحلق كله وسطه و اعلاه واسفله والاصل فيه قوله عليه السلام الذكاة ما بين اللبة واللحيين ولانه مجمع المجرى والعروق فيحصل بالفعل
He said: 'Slaughter is between the throat and the collar.' And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'There is no harm in slaughter in the entire throat: its middle, its top, and its bottom.' The basis for this is his saying (peace be upon him): 'The valid slaughter (al-dhakah) is between the collar and the jawbones.' And because it is the meeting place of the passage and the veins, so it is accomplished by the action.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage defines the permissible area for slaughter (dhabh) of animals. According to the author, the slaughter must be performed between the throat (al-halq) and the collar (al-labbah) — the upper part of the chest where the neck meets the body. Al-Jami' al-Saghir adds that it is permissible to slaughter anywhere in the throat: the middle, the top, or the bottom. The proof is the hadith of the Prophet: 'al-dhakah ma bayna al-labbah wa al-lahyayn' (the valid slaughter is between the collar and the jawbones). The rationale is that this area contains the major veins and the windpipe, so cutting there ensures proper bleeding and death. The final phrase 'fayahsulu bi al-fi'l' seems to be cut off but likely means that the act of slaughter accomplishes the purpose.

Key Terms
الذكاة (al-dhakah): The Islamic method of slaughter that renders the animal permissible to eat.
اللبة (al-labbah): The upper part of the chest where the neck meets the torso; the collar.
اللحيان (al-lahyayn): The two jawbones (the mandibles) — the upper limit of the cut.

Key Terms from Source Passage

The valid slaughter area is between the collar and jawbones; the entire throat within this range is acceptable, and this is proven by the Prophet's hadith.

The Condition of Pure Dhikr for Tasmiyah
Ruling with proof: condition of pure dhikr for tasmiyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for tasmiyah at slaughter, apply the requirement of pure dhikr to various utterances, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi difference on sneezing.

Core Idea
Tasmiyah must be pure dhikr without supplication; intention determines validity.

The Hanafi school insists that the tasmiyah at slaughter be a pure act of remembrance (dhikr khalis), free from any personal request. This lesson explores the boundary between acceptable forms of Allah's remembrance and those mixed with supplication, including a subtle intra-school difference regarding a sneeze.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core condition
The passage states that tasmiyah must be 'pure isolated remembrance' (al-dhikr al-khalis al-mujarrad). This is derived from the Companion Ibn Mas'ud's instruction: 'Make the tasmiyah pure.'
2
Examine the examples
Two positive examples: saying 'Alhamdulillah' or 'Subhanallah' with the intention of tasmiyah is valid. Two negative examples: saying 'Allahumma ighfir li' (a supplication) is invalid; sneezing and saying 'Alhamdulillah' without tasmiyah intention is invalid in the correct narration.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
There is a difference regarding the sneeze: the more correct of the two narrations (asahh al-riwayatayn) rules it invalid because the intention is praise for the sneeze, not tasmiyah.
4
Connect to the textual proof
The verse 'So mention the name of Allah upon them when lined up' (22:36), interpreted by Ibn Abbas, establishes the obligation. The Hanafi school reads the imperative as wajib, and the companion's practice specifies the form.
5
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
If a person says 'Bismillah' but with the intention of seeking blessing rather than the required dhikr, the meat is still halal because the utterance itself is pure dhikr. The key is the purity of the expression, not merely the intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشرط هو الذكر الخالص المجرد على ما قال ابن مسعود رضى الله عنه جردوا التسمية حتى لو قال عند الذبح اللهم اغفر لى لا يحل لانه دعاء وسوال ولو قال الحمد لله او سبحان الله يريد التسمية حل ولو عطس عند الذبح فقال الحمد لله لا يصح في اصح الروايتين لانه يريد به الحمد لله على نعمة دون التسمية وما تداولته الألسنة عند الذبح وهو قوله بسم الله والله اكبر منقول عن ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما في قوله تعالى فاذكروا اسم الله عليها صواف
And the condition is pure isolated remembrance, as Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'Make the tasmiyah pure.' So much so that if one says at the time of slaughter: 'O Allah, forgive me,' it is not permissible because it is a supplication and request. And if one says: 'Praise be to Allah' or 'Glory be to Allah' intending tasmiyah, it is permissible. And if one sneezes at the time of slaughter and says: 'Praise be to Allah,' it is not valid in the more correct of the two narrations, because he intends thereby praise of Allah for a blessing, not tasmiyah. And what is current on tongues at slaughter, which is his saying 'Bismillah Allahu Akbar,' is transmitted from Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them both) in explanation of the saying of Allah Most High: 'So mention the name of Allah upon them when lined up.'
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Qur'an (through the statement of Ibn Abbas) and from the practice of the Companions. The ruling: for the meat to be halal, the slaughterer must utter the tasmiyah (Bismillah) as a pure act of remembrance, without mixing it with any personal supplication or request. The author uses the example: if one says 'O Allah, forgive me' instead of 'Bismillah', the meat is not halal because it is a du'a (supplication), not pure dhikr. However, if one says 'Alhamdulillah' or 'Subhanallah' with the intention of tasmiyah, it qualifies. A separate case: if one sneezes at the time of slaughter and says 'Alhamdulillah' in response to the sneeze, the more correct view is that it does not count as tasmiyah, because the intention is praise for the blessing of the sneeze, not the required dhikr. The author also notes that the common formula 'Bismillah Allahu Akbar' is reported from Ibn Abbas as the interpretation of the Qur'anic verse. The Hanafi school emphasizes that the tasmiyah must be a direct act of remembrance, not incidental.

Key Terms
تسمية (tasmiyah): Uttering the name of Allah (Bismillah) at the time of slaughter, which is a condition for the meat to be halal.
ذكر خالص (dhikr khalis): Pure remembrance of Allah, free from any addition of personal supplication or request.
اصح الروايتين (asahh al-riwayatayn): The more correct of the two narrations within the Hanafi school, indicating a preferred position when there is disagreement in transmitted rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Tasmiyah must be a pure remembrance of Allah without supplication, and the validity depends on both the utterance form and the intention to fulfill the obligation.

Location of Slaughter: Between Throat and Chest
Specifying the permitted area for slaughter between the throat and the upper chest

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the valid area for slaughter in Hanafi fiqh and identify the hadith that defines its boundaries.

Core Idea
The entire throat from the upper chest to the jaw is valid for slaughter; the Prophet defined it explicitly.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on where exactly the cut must be made during animal slaughter. The school relies on a clear hadith defining the boundaries. While there is a recommended spot, the entire region is permissible. Understanding this removes common confusion about precise placement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is dhabh (slaughter). The ruling: valid if the cut falls within the specified throat area.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the Prophet's hadith: 'Al-dhakatu ma bayna al-labbati wa al-lahyayn'.
The hadith is ahad but its meaning is clear.
3
Explain the wisdom
The throat is where the major veins and passages converge; cutting there ensures rapid blood drainage.
This makes any cut in that area effective.
4
Note the comprehensiveness
Al-Jami' al-Saghir explicitly permits the middle, upper, and lower parts of the throat.
No distinction in ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال والذبح بين الحلق واللبة وفى الجامع الصغير لاباس بالدبح في الحلق كله وسطه و اعلاه واسفله والاصل فيه قوله عليه السلام الذكاة ما بين اللبة واللحيين ولانه مجمع المجرى والعروق فيحصل بالفعل فيه انها رالدم على ابلغ الوجوه فكان حكم الكل سواء
He said: And the slaughter is between the throat and the upper chest. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: There is no harm in slaughtering in the entire throat, its middle, its upper part, and its lower part. And the basis for this is his saying (peace be upon him): 'The lawful slaughter is between the upper chest and the two jawbones.' And because it is the meeting point of the passage and the veins, so by acting there, the blood is let out in the most complete manner. Thus the ruling for all is the same.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on the permissible location for slaughter (dhabh). According to the Hanafi school, the valid area for slaughter is the throat (halq) region, specifically from the upper chest (labbah) to the base of the jaw (lahyayn). The author cites a hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Al-dhakatu ma bayna al-labbati wa al-lahyayn' (The valid slaughter is between the upper chest and the two jawbones). The author also references al-Jami' al-Saghir by Imam Muhammad, which permits slaughter anywhere in the throat—its middle, top, or bottom—because all these parts contain the veins and passageways that, when cut, effectively drain the blood. The ulama agree that the primary goal is to sever the jugular veins to cause rapid blood loss; as long as the cut is within this area, it is valid. Therefore, whether one cuts high or low on the throat makes no difference to the permissibility of the meat.

Key Terms
حلق (halq): The throat; the specific area of the neck where slaughter is performed.
لبة (labbah): The upper chest or the base of the neck where the two collar bones meet (the jugular notch).
لحيان (lahyayn): The two jawbones (mandibles); the upper boundary of the valid slaughter area.

Key Terms from Source Passage

The Four Veins of Slaughter: Intra-Hanafi and Cross-Madhhab Analysis
Specifying the four veins and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four veins required for halal slaughter, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and compare the Hanafi position with the Shafi'i and Maliki schools.

Core Idea
Cutting three specific veins is required; the Sahibayn's view is the mufta bihi.

The ruling on halal slaughter hinges on which vessels are severed. This passage from Al-Hidayah delineates the four veins and presents proofs from hadith. An intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf emerges: Imam Abu Hanifa permits sacrificing most of the four, while the Sahibayn insist on cutting the windpipe, esophagus, and at least one jugular. Understanding this ikhtilaf clarifies the operative rule for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four veins
Name the halqum (windpipe), mari' (esophagus), and the two wadajan (jugular veins). Recognize that the hadith 'Afr al-awdaj' refers to these collectively.
The Arabic term 'awdaj' is a plural form whose minimum connotation is three.
2
Analyze the hadith evidence
The imperative 'afri' indicates obligation (wujub). The word 'awdaj' in the command implies at least three must be cut. Since the windpipe is automatically severed when cutting the other three, it is included by necessity.
The hadith is ahad, so it yields zanni thubut but qati dalala in Hanafi usul.
3
Map the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Abu Hanifa: cutting most of the four veins (e.g., three out of four completely, or a majority of each) suffices. The Sahibayn: must cut the halqum, mari', and exactly one wadaj. The passage says: 'Cutting most of them suffices according to Abu Hanifa. But the two said: There is no alternative to cutting the windpipe, esophagus, and one jugular.'
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of the Sahibayn due to its clarity and balance. It avoids the ambiguity of 'most' and ensures proper blood drainage.
Al-Marghinani favors this view in Al-Hidayah.
5
Compare with other schools
Shafi'i requires only halqum and mari'. Maliki requires all four. The Hanafi school (Sahibayn) requires three specific veins. The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) affects the validity of slaughter in each school.
The proof against Shafi'i: the hadith explicitly mentions 'awdaj' (jugulars), which they ignore.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال والعروق التي تقطع في الذكاة اربعة الحلقوم والمرى والودجان لقوله عليه السلام افر الأوادج بما شئت وهى اسم جمع واقلها الثلث فيتناول المرى والودجين وهو حجة على الشافعي في الاكتفاء بالحلقوم والمرى الا انه لا يمكن قطع هذه الثلثة الا بقطع الحلقوم فيثبت قطع الحلقوم باقتضائه وبظاهر ما ذكرنا يحتج مالك ولا يجوز الاكثر منها بل يشترط قطع جميعها وعندنا ان قطعها حل الأكل وإن قطع اكثرها فكذالك عند ابي حنيفة وقالا لا بد من قطع الحلقوم والمرى واحد الودجين
He said: And the veins that are cut in slaughter are four: the windpipe (halqum), the esophagus (mari'), and the two jugular veins (wadajan). Because of his saying (peace be upon him): 'Sever the jugular veins as you wish.' And it is a plural noun, and its minimum is three, so it includes the esophagus and the two jugulars. And it is an argument against al-Shafi'i in his sufficing with the windpipe and the esophagus. However, it is not possible to cut these three except by cutting the windpipe, so cutting the windpipe is established by implication. And by the apparent meaning of what we mentioned, Malik argues, and he does not permit [eating] if most of them are cut, rather he stipulates cutting all of them. And with us, if they are cut, eating is permissible; and if most of them are cut, it is likewise according to Abu Hanifa. But the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) said: There is no alternative to cutting the windpipe, the esophagus, and one of the two jugulars.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage addresses a central issue in the fiqh of dhabh: how many veins must be cut for the meat to be halal? The text first states the general position that the four veins to be cut are: the windpipe (halqum), the esophagus (mari'), and the two jugular veins (wadajan). The proof is the Prophet's command 'Afr al-awdaj' (Sever the jugular veins). Since 'awdaj' is a plural of minimum three (in some analyses), it implies at least three veins must be cut: the esophagus and the two jugulars. The windpipe is necessarily cut when these are severed. This hadith is also used as evidence against Imam al-Shafi'i, who only requires cutting the windpipe and esophagus. Imam Malik requires all four to be cut. Within the Hanafi school, Imam Abu Hanifa holds that cutting most of the four veins suffices—i.e., if more than half of each vein is cut, the slaughter is valid. The two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) require specific three: the windpipe, the esophagus, and at least one jugular vein. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is that of the Sahibayn, as it is more precautionary and practically applicable. The practical consequence: according to Abu Hanifa, cutting three out of four veins (or even 2.5?) is valid, whereas according to Sahibayn, you must ensure the three specific ones are cut. In fatwa practice, the Sahibayn's view is preferred.

Key Terms
حلقوم (halqum): The windpipe (trachea); the passage for air.
مرىء (mari'): The esophagus; the passage for food.
ودجان (wadajan): The two jugular veins (one on each side of the neck).
أوداج (awdaj): Plural of wadaj; the four jugular veins (or sometimes the group of four veins).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (Sahibayn), which veins must be cut for the animal to be halal?
A) All four veins (halqum, mari', and both wadajan)
B) The windpipe (halqum) and esophagus (mari') only
C) The windpipe (halqum), esophagus (mari'), and at least one jugular vein (wadaj)
D) Any three of the four veins

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi requires cutting the windpipe, esophagus, and at least one jugular vein for halal slaughter.

The Four Veins of Slaughter and the Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on required veins for dhabh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four veins involved in dhabh, explain why three are sufficient according to the Hanafi school, and distinguish the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
The hadith 'Afr al-awdaj' requires at least three veins; Hanafis agree on three but differ on which ones.

This lesson examines the classical Hanafi ruling on the veins that must be cut during Islamic slaughter (dhabh). The source passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah first identifies the four veins: the windpipe (halqum), esophagus (mari'), and the two jugular veins (wadajan). It then derives from the hadith 'Afr al-awdaj bima shi't' that cutting three is sufficient, because the plural 'awdaj' has a minimum of three. However, a well-known intra-Hanafi dispute arises: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that any three veins suffice, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) require the halqum, mari', and one wadaj. Some later authorities attribute this stricter view to Abu Yusuf alone. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for correctly applying the school's ruling in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four veins and their definitions
Name the four veins: al-halqum (windpipe, for breathing), al-mari' (esophagus, for food/drink), and al-wadajan (dual, the two jugular veins on the front of the neck). These are the vessels that must be cut for valid dhabh.
The passage notes the halqum is the breathing pipe and the mari' is the swallowing pipe.
2
Analyse the hadith evidence and its grammatical implication
The hadith 'Afr al-awdaj' uses the plural 'awdaj'. In Arabic, the indefinite plural (jam' taksir) indicates a minimum of three. Therefore, the hadith commands cutting at least three of these veins.
Some authorities read 'afri' (from the simple verb) meaning 'cut' rather than 'cut for corruption'.
3
Understand how the halqum is included by necessity
The mari' and the two wadajs cannot be cut without also cutting the halqum, which lies adjacent. Hence, the halqum is necessarily included, making the total four veins, but only three need to be intentionally cut.
This reasoning refutes the Shafi'i position that two suffice and the Maliki that all four are required.
4
State the Hanafi consensus: three veins suffice
All Hanafi authorities agree that cutting three of the four veins renders the meat halal. The dispute is only over which three.
No Hanafi requires all four; the school is unanimous on the number three.
5
Distinguish the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that any three veins (without specification) are sufficient. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) require the halqum, mari', and one wadaj. Some later texts attribute the companions' view to Abu Yusuf alone.
The mufta bihi is the view of Imam Abu Hanifa (any three suffice).

یعنی ابو یوسف تین پر اکتفا کو جائز کہتے ہیں لیکن اس شرط کیساتھ کہ دو جان میں سے ایک پر اکتفا کیا جائے اور باقی سب کو کاٹا جائے اور
Book of Slaughter. In it, that the blood is let out in the most complete manner, so the ruling of all is the same. Quduri translated that slaughter is between the throat and the chest, and in al-Jami' al-Saghir there is no harm in slaughtering in the entire throat, its middle, its upper part, and its lower part. The basis for this is the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Slaughter is between the chest and the two jaws.' And because the throat is the meeting point of the pipes and veins, working at the meeting point achieves the complete flow of blood, so the ruling of all is the same. Explanation: This issue has passed before; the hadith is clear. Here only this much is worth explaining: any part of the throat, whether upper, middle, or lower, wherever slaughter is performed, it is equal, because the main purpose is to separate the blood from the meat, and this purpose is fulfilled from every place. The place of flow which I translated as 'nali' (pipe). In the throat, one pipe is where food goes, and one pipe is where breath is taken. Which veins must be cut in slaughter? He said: The veins that are cut in slaughter are four: al-halqum, al-mari', and al-wadajan. Due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Cut the awdaj with whatever you wish.' It is a collective noun, and its minimum is three, so it includes al-mari' and the two wadajs. This is a proof against al-Shafi'i who is satisfied with cutting al-halqum and al-mari'. But it is not possible to cut these three except by cutting al-halqum, so cutting al-halqum is established by implication. By the apparent meaning of what we have mentioned, Malik argues, and he does not permit most of them, but rather requires cutting all of them. According to us, if all are cut, eating is lawful. And if most of them are cut, then similarly according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: It is necessary to cut al-halqum, al-mari', and one of the two wadajs. Translation: Quduri said that the veins which are cut in slaughter are four: halqum, mari', and the two wadajs. Due to the command of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Cut the four veins with whatever you wish.' And this (awdaj) is a plural word, and its minimum is three, so it includes the mari' and both wadajs. This hadith is a proof against al-Shafi'i who is satisfied with al-halqum and al-mari'. But cutting these three is not possible except with cutting the halqum, so cutting the halqum is established by implication. By its apparent meaning, Malik argues, and he does not permit most of them, but rather requires cutting all of them. According to us, if all are cut, the meat becomes lawful. And if most of them are cut, then similarly according to Abu Hanifa. The two companions said: Cutting the halqum, mari', and one of the two wadajs is necessary. Explanation: The veins that are cut in slaughter are four: 1. Halqum, 2. Mari', 3-4. Wadajan. Halqum is originally 'halq' with an extra waw and mim, so it becomes 'halqum'. It is the windpipe. Mari' is the pipe where food and water go. Wadajan is the dual of wadaj. These are on the front part of the neck, between the halqum and mari'. In Urdu, mari' is called 'zukhra'. In any case, these are the four veins that are cut in slaughter. Now a hadith is presented: 'Afr al-awdaj bima shi't.' This 'awdaj' is a plural whose minimum is three, so it includes the mari' and the wadajs. Since these three cannot be cut without cutting the halqum, the halqum is also included by implication. Thus the total becomes four veins. When a hadith establishes four veins, this hadith is a proof against Imam al-Shafi'i because he said: 'Satisfying with cutting halqum and mari' is permissible because after cutting these two, the animal does not survive.' So Imam al-Shafi'i permitted satisfaction with two. Imam Malik, using the apparent meaning mentioned, declared cutting all four as necessary and said: 'If one vein remains, the slaughtered animal is not lawful.' As for the Hanafi issue, up to this point there is agreement that satisfaction with three out of these four is permissible. Then which three? In this, Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions differ. Imam Abu Hanifa says: 'Without specification, whatever three are cut, the animal is lawful.' The two companions said: 'Cutting the halqum and mari' is necessary, but if one of the wadajs is satisfied with, that is better.' Caution: 'Aqr' comes from the form if'al, but when it comes from if'al, its meaning is cutting for corruption, and when it comes from the simple triliteral, its meaning is absolute cutting, and that is appropriate. So reading it as 'afri' is more appropriate. Caution: 'Idraj' is not a collective noun but a plural, so here it is a noun of meaning. How many veins must be cut? The sayings of the jurists. He said, may Allah be pleased with him: This is how al-Quduri mentioned the difference in his Mukhtasar. The well-known in the books of our mashayikh, may Allah have mercy on them, is that this is the saying of Abu Yusuf alone. Translation: The author said that al-Quduri mentioned the difference in his Mukhtasar as such, and it is well-known in the books of our mashayikh that this is only the saying of Abu Yusuf. Explanation: The author of Hidayah says that Imam Quduri mentioned this same difference in his Mukhtasar which we have transmitted, but in the books of the mashayikh, it is famously transmitted that in this there is only difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf. That is, Abu Yusuf considers satisfaction with three permissible, but with the condition that one of the two wadajs is satisfied with, and the rest are cut.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah is a commentary on the section of slaughter (al-dhaba'ih) from al-Hidayah. It begins by discussing the location of slaughter on the throat, affirming that any part of the throat suffices because the purpose is to let out blood. Then it addresses the main legal issue: which veins must be cut for the slaughter to be valid. The Arabic matn states that the veins cut in slaughter are four: al-halqum (windpipe), al-mari' (esophagus), and al-wadajan (the two jugular veins). The proof is the hadith 'Afr al-awdaj bima shi't' - 'Cut the awdaj with whatever you wish.' The term 'awdaj' is interpreted as a plural with a minimum of three, so it includes the mari' and the two wadajs. By necessary implication, cutting the halqum is also required because these three cannot be cut without it. Thus four veins are established. This is presented as a proof against Imam al-Shafi'i, who held that cutting the halqum and mari' suffices. Imam Malik, on the other hand, argued from the apparent meaning that all four must be cut. Within the Hanafi school, there is agreement that cutting three of the four is sufficient. However, a difference arises between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Imam Abu Hanifa holds that any three veins suffice without specification. The two companions require that the halqum, mari', and one of the wadajs must be cut. This difference has practical implications: under Abu Hanifa's view, if the three cut veins are not specifically the halqum and mari', the slaughter is still valid; under the view of the two companions, the halqum and mari' must be among those cut. The author also notes a variant narration from al-Quduri, which attributes the view of requiring specific three to Abu Yusuf alone, not both companions. This highlights the care needed in transmitting legal positions within the school.

Key Terms
حلقوم (al-halqum): The windpipe; the breathing tube in the throat.
مرىء (al-mari'): The esophagus; the swallowing tube.
ودجان (al-wadajan): The dual of wadaj; the two jugular veins on the front of the neck.
أوداج (awdaj): Plural of wadaj; used in the hadith. The Hanafi interpretation takes the minimum of the plural as three.
ذكاة (dhakah): Proper Islamic slaughter of a permissible animal by cutting the throat veins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa regarding the number of veins that must be cut in dhabh?
A) All four veins must be cut.
B) Any three of the four veins suffice.
C) Only the halqum and mari' must be cut.
D) The halqum, mari', and both wadajs must be cut.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires three of the four veins in slaughter to be cut; Abu Hanifa allows any three, while the two companions require the windpipe, esophagus, and one jugular.
