intermediate
6 min
Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Number of Veins Required in Dhabh
Intra-Hanafi dispute on which three veins suffice in dhabh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the required veins in dhabh and identify the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
Three veins are sufficient for dhabh, but the Hanafi school is divided on which three: Abu Hanifa allows any three, while Abu Yusuf requires the trachea, esophagus, and one jugular vein.

Islamic slaughter (dhabh) requires cutting specific veins for the meat to be halal. A hadith provides the basis, but its interpretation leads to differing rulings within and outside the Hanafi school. This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf, focusing on how many and which veins must be severed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agreed ruling
All Hanafi scholars agree that three out of the four veins (trachea, esophagus, two jugulars) must be cut. This is the starting point.
2
Locate the point of disagreement
The dispute is over which three: Abu Hanifa says any three suffice; Abu Yusuf specifies the trachea, esophagus, and one jugular vein.
Imam Muhammad's view is not mentioned in this source.
3
Analyze the proof from hadith
The hadith 'Iqta' al-awdaj' is cited. The plural 'awdaj' implies at least two jugulars, and by necessity the trachea and esophagus are included, making four veins. Abu Yusuf's interpretation requires the trachea and esophagus plus one jugular.
The imperative 'iqta'' indicates obligation (wajib) in Hanafi usul.
4
Note the cross-madhhab comparison
The Shafi'i school requires only trachea and esophagus; the Maliki school requires all four. The Hanafi position is intermediate.
The hadith is used as proof against the Shafi'i view.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is Abu Yusuf's view: cutting the trachea, esophagus, and one jugular vein. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
Even though some sources attribute the view to both Sahibayn, the famous position is that it is only Abu Yusuf's.

Now he presents a hadith as proof: 'Cut the jugular veins as you wish.' 'Awdaj' is a plural whose minimum is two. Therefore, it includes the trachea and the two jugular veins. And since these three cannot be cut without the esophagus, by implication the esophagus is also included, and in this way all veins become four. When four veins are proven by the hadith, this hadith is an argument against Imam al-Shafi'i because he said that cutting the esophagus and trachea suffices, since the animal does not survive after both are cut. So Imam al-Shafi'i declared sufficiency with two, and Imam Malik, by using the apparent meaning, deemed cutting all four necessary and said that if any vein remains, the slaughtered animal is not halal. As for the Hanafi school, there is agreement that three out of these four suffice. Then, which three? There is a difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions. Imam Abu Hanifa says: without specification, whichever three are cut, the slaughtered animal is halal. The two companions said: cutting the esophagus and trachea is necessary, but if one of the two jugular veins is sufficient, that is better. Note: 'Aqir' comes from the form if'al, but when it comes from the form if'al, its meaning is to cut for corruption, and when it comes from the simple triliteral, its meaning is to cut absolutely, and that is appropriate; so reading it as 'ifri' is more suitable. Note: 'Idraj' is not a noun of a collective but a plural, so here it is a verbal noun. How many veins are necessary to cut? Statements of jurists. He (the author) said: Thus al-Quduri mentioned the difference in his Mukhtasar, and what is well-known in the books of our scholars is that this is only the statement of Abu Yusuf. Translation: The author said that al-Quduri mentioned the difference in his Mukhtasar as we have narrated, but in the books of our scholars it is famously transmitted that there is only a difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf. That is, Abu Yusuf allows sufficiency with three, but with the condition that one of the two jugular veins is sufficient and the rest are cut. Imam Abu Hanifa considers sufficiency with three absolutely, meaning the statement of Imam Muhammad is not mentioned in this.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal dispute regarding the number of veins that must be severed in Islamic slaughter (dhabh). The author begins by citing the hadith 'Iqta' al-awdaj bi ma shi'ta' (Cut the jugular veins as you wish), which is the primary evidence. The term 'awdaj' (plural of wadaj) linguistically indicates a minimum of two, thus including the two jugular veins (wadajayn). Since the trachea (halqum) and esophagus (mari') are necessarily cut along with these two, the total becomes four veins: the trachea, esophagus, and two jugular veins. This hadith serves as a proof against Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that cutting only the trachea and esophagus suffices because the animal dies. Imam Malik, in contrast, requires all four to be cut based on the apparent meaning of the text. Within the Hanafi school, there is agreement that three of the four veins suffice. The disagreement is over which three: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that any three are sufficient without specification, while the two companions (Sahibayn) require the trachea and esophagus to be cut, and then one of the two jugular veins is sufficient but better. The passage also includes a note about the verb 'aqara' (to cut), distinguishing between its form if'al (meaning to cut corruptingly) and the simple form (meaning absolute cutting). Another note clarifies that 'idraj' is a plural, not a collective noun. Finally, the author notes a discrepancy: al-Quduri attributes the view of requiring three with the condition to both companions, but according to the famous position in the books of the Hanafi scholars, this is only the view of Abu Yusuf, not including Imam Muhammad. Thus, the intra-Hanafi dispute is actually between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, with Abu Yusuf's final position requiring the trachea and esophagus plus one jugular vein.

Key Terms
حلقوم (halqum): The trachea or windpipe through which air passes.
مریء (mari'): The esophagus, the tube through which food and drink pass.
وداج (wadaj): Jugular vein; plural 'awdaj. The two major blood vessels on either side of the neck.
ذبیحہ (dhabihah): The animal slaughtered according to Islamic rites; the ruling of halal or haram depends on proper cutting of veins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Any three of the four veins without specification.
B) The trachea, esophagus, and one jugular vein.
C) All four veins.
D) Only the trachea and esophagus.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires three veins to be cut; the mufta bihi is the trachea, esophagus, and one jugular vein.

Cutting Half vs. Majority of Each Vein in Dhabh
Clarifying the required extent of cutting each vein in dhabh and the intra-Hanafi differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi positions on the required extent of cutting each vein in dhabh and explain the legal reasoning (talil) for requiring the majority of each vein.

Core Idea
Cutting only half of each vein is insufficient; the majority must be severed, as each vein is an independent entity.

In this passage, the author explains an intra-Hanafi dispute about how much of each vein must be cut for the dhabh to be valid. While Imam Muhammad's al-Jami' al-Saghir explicitly states that cutting the majority suffices, the earlier Hanafi masters differed on the exact number of veins and the extent required. This lesson unpacks the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad, focusing on the legal principle that the majority stands in for the whole.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling from al-Jami' al-Saghir
Imam Muhammad rules that cutting half of the trachea and half of the jugular veins is invalid, but cutting the majority before death is valid. Note that he does not report disagreement, though transmissions differ.
This sets the baseline: majority of each vein is sufficient.
2
Analyze the differing transmissions
Abu Hanifa initially held that any three of the four veins suffice without requiring majority of each; Abu Yusuf first agreed then retracted to requiring full cut of trachea, esophagus, and one jugular; Imam Muhammad insists on majority of each individual vein.
The dispute is both about the number of veins and the extent within each.
3
Understand the legal reasoning (talil)
Each vein is an independent entity (fard) because it is separate from others and the command to cut applies to each. Therefore, the majority of each must be cut, as the majority stands in for the whole (al-akthar yunazzalu manzilat al-kull).
This principle is central to the Hanafi position.
4
Apply the maxim to derive the requirement
Since the command is to cut each vein, cutting the majority of each satisfies the obligation. Cutting half does not fulfill the command because half is not equivalent to the whole.
The maxim operates on each vein individually.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The later Hanafi scholars (e.g., al-Hidayah) prefer the view that the majority of each vein is required, based on Imam Muhammad's narration and one from Abu Hanifa. This is the operative position for fatwa.
This resolves the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.

کا مناضروری نہیں ہے کیوں کہ چاروں رگیں الگ الگ ہیں دوسری سے جدا ہیں تو ہر ایک کو اصل شمار کیا جائے گا تو ہر ایک کو کا شناضروری ہوگا اور چونکہ حکم بنص مذکور فیما سبق ہر ایک کے قطع کا وارد ہوا ہے ( کما اوضحناہ) تو ہر ایک کا حصہ اکثر کا شناضروری ہے کیوں کہ اکثر کو کل کے قائم مقام کر دیا جاتا ہے
He said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'If he cuts half of the trachea and half of the jugular veins, it is not eaten. If he cuts the majority of the jugular veins and the trachea before the animal dies, it is eaten.' And he did not narrate any disagreement, but the transmissions differ on this. Translation: And Muhammad said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'If he cuts half of the trachea and half of the jugular veins, it will not be eaten. If he cuts the majority of the jugular veins and the trachea before death, it will be eaten.' And he did not narrate any disagreement, but the transmissions differ in this. Explanation: Imam Muhammad stated in al-Jami' al-Saghir what was mentioned, the meaning of which is that if he cuts each of the mentioned veins but only half of each, it is not halal; and if he cuts the majority, it is halal, and the cutting of the majority must occur before the animal dies. And Imam Muhammad did not narrate any disagreement in al-Jami' al-Saghir, even though the transmissions differ on the ruling of this issue. Now the author will present the conclusion of the mentioned details. How many veins must be cut according to Imam Abu Hanifa, Imam Abu Yusuf, and Imam Muhammad for the slaughtered animal to be considered halal? The conclusion is that according to Abu Hanifa, if he cuts three, any three, it becomes halal. And this was also the view of Abu Yusuf initially, then he retracted to what we mentioned. And from Muhammad, it is narrated that he considers the majority of each individual (vein), and this is a narration from Abu Hanifa, because each individual (vein) is an entity in itself due to its separation from others, and because the command to cut it has been issued, so the majority of each individual is considered. Explanation: The summary of this detail is that Imam Abu Hanifa's madhhab is that any three of the four veins, without specification, if cut, it is correct and the slaughter is halal. And this was also the initial view of Imam Abu Yusuf. Then Imam Abu Yusuf adopted the view that was mentioned: that cutting the trachea, esophagus, and one of the two jugular veins is necessary. Imam Abu Hanifa's madhhab is that all four must be cut, but if the majority of these four is cut, it is sufficient; the entire cutting of each is not necessary, because the four veins are separate, each distinct from the other, so each is considered an entity, and thus cutting each is necessary. And since the command, by the text mentioned previously, for cutting each has come down (as we have clarified), the majority part of each must be cut, because the majority is made to stand in for the whole.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage presents a further intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the required extent of cutting within each vein. It begins with a quotation from Imam Muhammad's al-Jami' al-Saghir: if only half of the trachea and half of the jugular veins are cut, the meat is not halal; but if the majority of these veins is cut before the animal dies, it is halal. Imam Muhammad did not record any disagreement in that work, yet there are differing transmissions on this point. The author then summarizes the positions: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that cutting any three of the four veins (without specifying which) suffices, and the entire cutting of each vein is not required; rather, the majority of each vein is considered sufficient. This is based on the principle that each vein is an independent entity, and the command to cut them applies individually. Since the majority stands in for the whole (a legal maxim), cutting the majority of each vein satisfies the requirement. Imam Abu Yusuf initially held the same view as Abu Hanifa but later retracted to requiring the trachea, esophagus, and one jugular vein to be cut entirely (not just majority). Imam Muhammad's position as narrated is that the majority of each individual vein must be cut, which is also a narration from Abu Hanifa. Thus, the key ruling is that cutting only half of a vein is insufficient; the majority must be severed before the animal's death. The lesson underscores the Hanafi legal reasoning (talil) that each vein is a separate entity and the command to cut them applies to each, so the majority of each is required.

Key Terms
اکثر (akthar): Majority; more than half of each vein. Used to indicate the sufficient amount to cut.
فرد (fard): Individual; each vein is considered a separate entity (fard) with its own ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The meat is halal because half is considered sufficient.
B) The meat is not halal (not eaten).
C) The meat is halal only if the animal dies immediately.
D) The ruling depends on the intention of the slaughterer.

Key Takeaway
The majority of each required vein must be cut in dhabh; half is insufficient.

Minimum Veins for Halal Slaughter: Three of Four Suffice
Explains the ruling when three of the four veins are cut during slaughter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cutting three of the four jugular veins for slaughter and explain the proof based on the principle of majority, including the intra-Hanafi objection of Imam Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Cutting three of the four jugular veins suffices; the majority takes the ruling of the whole.

Islamic slaughter requires severing certain veins in the neck to ensure rapid death and proper blood drainage. The Hanafi school specifies four veins: the windpipe (halqum), esophagus (mari'), and the two jugular veins (wadajayn). This lesson examines whether cutting only three is sufficient, as held by Imam Abu Hanifa and relied upon in the school, and addresses a caution from Imam Abu Yusuf who required all four.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its purpose
Determine the act under discussion: cutting the neck of an animal for slaughter. Understand the shar'i purpose (maqsad): quick death and blood drainage.
The text says: 'مقصود خون نکالنا ہے اور یہ ہے کہ روح جلدی نکل جائے'
2
State the ruling on cutting three veins
The text rules that cutting three of the four veins is permissible (jā'iz) and the meat is halal. This is the relied-upon Hanafi position.
The author says: 'تین پر اکتفا جائز ہے'
3
Analyze the proof: principle of majority
The proof is based on the principle that the majority takes the ruling of the whole (al-akthar lahu hukm al-kull). Three veins constitute the majority, so the slaughter is valid.
The text also discusses the counterexample from Surah Fatiha to show this principle is not universal.
4
Consider the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Abu Yusuf holds that all four must be cut, as the purpose is not just blood flow but a specific shar'i act (dhaka shar'iyya). His view is considered more precautionary (ahwat).
The text notes: 'امام ابو یوسف کا قول احوط معلوم ہوتا ہے'
5
Apply the ruling: what if only two are cut?
If only two veins are cut, the majority is not severed, so it is as if nothing was cut. The meat is not halal.
The text explains the preference for prohibition when two aspects conflict.

مقصود خون نکالنا ہے اور یہ ہے کہ روح جلدی نکل جائے اور یہ مقصد حاصل ہے کیوں کہ جب گھاس یا سانس کی نالی کٹ گئی تو اب جانور زندہ نہیں رہے گا اور دونوں و رج میں سے جون سی کٹ گئی تو خون سارا نکل جائے گا تو تین پر اکتفا جائز ہے تا کہ جانور کو زیادہ تکلیف نہ ہو سکے۔ ہاں اگر چار میں سے دور گئیں کائیں تو اب ذبیحہ حلال نہ ہوگا کیوں کہ اکثر کو نہیں کا نا تو گویا اس نے کچھ نہیں کاٹا۔ سوال آدھی رگیں کاٹ چکا ہے تو کچھ نہ کاٹنے کا کیا مطلب؟ جواب یہاں دو پہلو جمع ہو گئے ایک حلت کا اور ایک حرمت کا، اور حرمت کے پہلو کوترجیح ہوئی ہے اس وجہ سے حرمت کے پہلو کو ترجیح دیتے ہوئے کہا گیا ہے کہ کچھ نہیں کا ٹا۔ تنبیہ امام ابو یوسف کا قول احوط معلوم ہوتا ہے کیوں کہ روح اور خون نکالنا ہی مقصود نہیں یہاں تک کہ بکری کے دو ٹکڑے کرنے سے بھی یہ مقصود حاصل ہوسکتا تھا بلکہ ذکاۃ شرعی مقصود ہے اور وہ اوراج ہیں اور اوراج میان بر بناء تغلیب چاروں داخل ہیں (كما صرح في الدر المختار ) تو بہتر تو یہ ہے کہ چاروں کو کاٹا جائے لیکن اگر تین پر اکتفا ہو حلقوم اور مرئی میں مخالفت کی وجہ سے ان کو الگ الگ اعتبار کیا جائے گا اور ود جان میں ایک دوسری کی نیابت کر سکتی ہے اور امام صاحب نے جو یہ کل یہ ارشاد فرمایا کہ اکثر کے لئے کل کا حکم ہے تو یہ کوئی قاعدہ کلیہ نہیں ورنہ سورہ فاتحہ کی سات آیتوں میں سے پانچ پر اکتفا جائز ہونا چاہیئے ۔ حالانکہ ہوا ایسا کرنے سے سجدہ سہو لازم آتا ہے بہر حال جہاں دلیل نہ ہو وہاں اکثر کو کل کے قائم مقام نہیں کیا جاسکتا۔
The purpose is to extract blood so the spirit leaves quickly. This purpose is achieved because when the windpipe or esophagus is cut, the animal will not survive, and when one of the two jugular veins is cut, blood will flow entirely. So relying on three is permissible to avoid extra pain. Yes, if only two of the four are cut, the slaughtered animal will not be halal because the majority is not cut, so it is as if nothing was cut. Question: He has cut half of the veins, so what is the meaning of 'as if nothing was cut'? Answer: Here two aspects are combined: one of permissibility and one of prohibition, and the aspect of prohibition is given preference, so it is said that he has cut nothing. Caution: The statement of Imam Abu Yusuf appears more precautionary because the purpose is not merely extracting spirit and blood, to the extent that cutting a goat into two pieces could achieve this purpose; rather, shari slaughter (dhaka shari'yya) is the purpose, and that is the four veins, and the four veins are included in the majority based on the principle of inclusion (taghlib). So it is better to cut all four. But if one relies on three, the windpipe (halqum) and esophagus (mari') are considered separately due to their difference, and the two jugular veins can substitute for one another. And Imam Sahib's statement that the majority has the ruling of the whole is not a universal principle; otherwise, reciting five of the seven verses of Surah Fatiha should be sufficient, whereas doing so necessitates sajda al-sahw. In any case, where there is no proof, the majority cannot take the place of the whole.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This is a ruling passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah discussing the minimum requirement for cutting the jugular veins in Islamic slaughter. The author first explains the purpose: to cause blood flow and quick death. Cutting three out of the four veins (windpipe, esophagus, and one jugular) is sufficient because the purpose is achieved. However, if only two are cut, the majority is not severed, so the animal is not halal. A question and answer clarify that when half the veins are cut, there are two conflicting aspects, and prohibition is given preference, making it as if nothing was cut. A tanbeeh notes that Imam Abu Yusuf's view is more precautionary: he holds that all four veins must be cut because the purpose is not just blood flow but a shari act of slaughter. Nevertheless, the relied-upon Hanafi position allows three. The principle that the majority takes the ruling of the whole is then qualified with a counterexample from Surah Fatiha, showing it is not a universal rule but applies only where there is no specific proof to the contrary.

Key Terms
اوداج (awdaj): The four canals of the neck that must be severed for valid slaughter: the windpipe (halqum), esophagus (mari'), and the two jugular veins (wadajayn).
حلقوم (halqum): The trachea, one of the four veins to be cut.
مری (mari'): The gullet, one of the four veins to be cut.
ورید (warid): The jugular vein or carotid artery, two of the four veins to be cut.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, how many of the four neck veins must be cut for the slaughter to be halal?
A) All four
B) At least three
C) At least two
D) All four, as per Imam Abu Yusuf

Key Takeaway
Cutting three of the four jugular veins suffices for halal slaughter in the Hanafi school, based on the principle that the majority achieves the purpose.
